You are a MASTER EDITOR and biblical scholar of the highest caliber, comparable to Robert Alter, James Kugel, Harold Bloom, or Ellen F. Davis—but you're also a GIFTED TEACHER who knows how to make complex ideas fascinating and accessible.

You have been provided with:

Your task: Review the introduction and verse commentary critically, identify weaknesses, and revise/rewrite to achieve EXCELLENCE for a specific audience.

**YOUR AUDIENCE: Intelligent First-Year College Student**

You are writing for a bright first-year college student taking a survey course in Biblical poetry. This student:
- **DOES have excellent Hebrew proficiency** (biblical, rabbinic, and liturgical Hebrew) — so you can and should quote Hebrew freely with English translation
- **IS NOT a scholar** and is NOT familiar with academic jargon, literary terminology, or scholarly conventions

- **WANTS to understand the "why" behind things** — not just what the text says, but why it matters, why the poet chose these words, what makes this interesting

Think of yourself as the coolest professor in the department—the one whose classes students actually want to attend. You:
- **Make complex ideas clear without dumbing them down**
- **Use concrete examples and vivid analogies** to illustrate abstract points
- **Define every technical term immediately** in plain English (don't assume prior knowledge)
- **Engage your reader directly** — it's okay to say "Notice how..." or "Here's the interesting part..."
- **Occasionally use humor** when appropriate (a well-placed observation, a clever comparison)
- **Show enthusiasm for the text** without being breathless or hyperbolic
- **Write like you're having a conversation with a smart friend** over coffee, not lecturing in a stuffy seminar room

### INTRODUCTION ESSAY (for review)
# Introduction to Psalm 8

Psalm 8 opens with a liturgical mystery: "For the conductor, upon the *gittit*" (לַמְנַצֵּ֥חַ עַֽל־הַגִּתִּ֗ית). This cryptic superscription—shared only with Psalms 81 and 84—has puzzled commentators for millennia. Rashi suggests a musical instrument from Gath, while Ibn Ezra proposes it was composed when David dwelt in that Philistine city. The Septuagint's rendering "concerning the winepresses" (*hyper tōn lēnōn*) hints at a harvest festival setting. Yet this obscure technical detail prepares us for the psalm's central paradox: the juxtaposition of the vast and the minute, the cosmic and the intimate, divine transcendence and human dignity.

The psalm's architecture is deceptively simple: an *inclusio* (envelope structure) of praise frames a meditation on humanity's place in creation. "יְהֹוָ֤ה אֲדֹנֵ֗ינוּ מָה־אַדִּ֣יר שִׁ֭מְךָ בְּכׇל־הָאָ֑רֶץ" ("O LORD, our Lord, how majestic is Your name throughout the earth!" vv. 2, 10) bookends the composition. Between these twin declarations, the psalmist constructs a theological argument through careful escalation: from cosmic vastness (vv. 2-4) to human smallness (v. 5) to humanity's unexpected exaltation (vv. 6-9). This movement—heaven to earth to dominion—reverses the trajectory of Genesis 1, where God's creative work culminates in humanity. Here, the poet begins with the finished cosmos and works backward to ask: What is humanity's role in all this?

The opening gesture is audacious. After the initial praise, verse 2 makes a startling claim: "אֲשֶׁ֥ר תְּנָ֥ה ה֝וֹדְךָ֗ עַל־הַשָּׁמָֽיִם" ("You who have set Your splendor above the heavens"). The verb *tenah* (תְּנָה) is grammatically difficult—possibly imperative "set!" or perfect "You have set"—but the theological move is clear: God's glory transcends even the heavens themselves. Yet immediately follows verse 3's jarring contrast: "מִפִּ֤י עוֹלְלִ֨ים ׀ וְֽיֹנְקִים֮ יִסַּ֢דְתָּ֫ עֹ֥ז" ("Out of the mouths of infants and sucklings You have founded strength"). The cosmic deity who crowns the heavens with splendor also founds *oz* (עֹז)—variously translated as "strength," "fortress," or "praise"—through the most vulnerable human voices. This is the psalm's governing paradox: divine power manifested through human weakness.

The phrase "founded strength from the mouths of babes" (*yissadta oz mipi ololim v'yonqim*) resists straightforward interpretation. Rashi reads it messianically, connecting it to the Temple cult where Levites and priests, "raised in filth" like infants, offer praise. The New Testament appropriates verse 3 differently: when Jesus cleanses the Temple and children cry "*Hosanna*," he cites this verse as scriptural warrant (Matthew 21:16). But within the psalm's own logic, this image of infant speech functions as the first term in a series of inversions. If God can establish a fortress through baby-talk, what else might divine power accomplish through unlikely agents?

The psalm's central rhetorical question arrives in verse 5: "מָה־אֱנ֥וֹשׁ כִּֽי־תִזְכְּרֶ֑נּוּ וּבֶן־אָ֝דָ֗ם כִּ֣י תִפְקְדֶֽנּוּ" ("What is *enosh* that You remember him, and *ben-adam* that You take note of him?"). The synonymous parallelism—*enosh* (אֱנוֹשׁ) and *ben-adam* (בֶן־אָדָם)—layers two Hebrew terms for humanity, the first emphasizing frailty and mortality, the second earthiness and creatureliness. Job 7:17-18 later inverts this verse bitterly: "What is man that You magnify him... that You inspect him every morning?" For Job, divine attention becomes oppressive scrutiny; for Psalm 8, it is sheer wonder. The verbs *tizkerenu* (תִזְכְּרֶנּוּ, "remember") and *tifqedenu* (תִפְקְדֶֽנּוּ, "take note of") carry covenantal freight in biblical Hebrew. The concordance reveals that *paqad* (פקד) frequently signifies not casual attention but purposeful visitation—whether for blessing (Genesis 21:1, God "remembered" Sarah) or judgment (Exodus 32:34, God will "punish" sin). Here, divine mindfulness implies active, caring engagement.

The answer to "what is man?" comes in verse 6's famous crux: "וַתְּחַסְּרֵ֣הוּ מְּ֭עַט מֵאֱלֹהִ֑ים" ("Yet You made him little less than *elohim*"). This single word, *me-elohim* (מֵאֱלֹהִ֑ים), generates centuries of debate. Does it mean "less than God" or "less than angels"? The lexical evidence is ambiguous: *elohim* can denote the one God, multiple gods, divine beings, or human judges (Exodus 22:8). The Septuagint chose "angels" (*angelous*), influencing Hebrews 2:7's christological reading. Rashi follows this angelic interpretation, citing Moses's ascent to heaven and Joshua's power over sun and moon as examples of human beings temporarily granted angelic-level authority. Ibn Ezra, characteristically rationalist, argues for "angels" on grammatical grounds: humanity's spirit (*neshama*) is of angelic quality but diminished by embodiment. Radak synthesizes: humanity is "little lower than *elohim*"—the angels—precisely because the human spirit, though incorporeal like theirs, dwells in a body.

Yet the psalm's own logic suggests we need not resolve this ambiguity definitively. The point is humanity's proximity to the divine realm, whether "almost-God" or "almost-angels." Verse 6 continues with a remarkable coronation: "וְכָב֖וֹד וְהָדָ֣ר תְּעַטְּרֵֽהוּ" ("and You crown him with glory and majesty"). The verb *te'atrenu* (תְּעַטְּרֵֽהוּ, "You crown") appears only here and in Psalm 65:12, where God "crowns the year" with goodness. The nouns *kavod* (כָּבוֹד, "glory") and *hadar* (הָדָ֣ר, "majesty")—typically reserved for God (Psalm 96:6: "Splendor and majesty are before Him")—are here bestowed on humanity. This is the psalm's theological revolution: the democratization of royal ideology. In Ancient Near Eastern thought, only the king bore the divine image and exercised cosmic dominion. Egyptian coronation texts declare Pharaoh "the image of Re" who "tramples the foreign lands." Mesopotamian royal inscriptions depict kings as divine agents subduing chaos. Psalm 8 explodes this monarchical monopoly: *every* human being is crowned with glory, *all* humanity exercises dominion.

The dominion catalog (vv. 7-9) specifies this authority: "תַּ֭מְשִׁילֵהוּ בְּמַעֲשֵׂ֣י יָדֶ֑יךָ כֹּ֝֗ל שַׁ֣תָּה תַֽחַת־רַגְלָֽיו" ("You have made him master over Your handiwork, laying all things at his feet"). The royal footstool imagery—"under his feet" (*taḥat raglav*)—echoes Ancient Near Eastern conquest iconography where defeated enemies lie prostrate beneath the king's feet. But here the subjected entities are not foreign nations but "צֹנֶ֣ה וַאֲלָפִ֣ים כֻּלָּ֑ם וְ֝גַ֗ם בַּהֲמ֥וֹת שָׂדָֽי" ("flocks and cattle, all of them, and also the beasts of the field," v. 8)—domestic animals followed by wild creatures, then "צִפּ֣וֹר שָׁ֭מַיִם וּדְגֵ֣י הַיָּ֑ם" ("birds of the heavens and fish of the sea," v. 9). This three-realm catalog—land, sky, sea—maps precisely onto Genesis 1:26-28's grant of dominion. Yet Psalm 8 adds a poetic flourish: "עֹ֝בֵ֗ר אׇרְח֥וֹת יַמִּֽים" ("whatever travels the paths of the seas"). The phrase "paths of the seas" (*orḥot yammim*) is extraordinary—it implies navigable routes through chaotic waters, order imposed on formlessness. Humanity's dominion extends even to mastering the sea's trackless depths.

Significantly, the psalm shifts from "fingers" (*etzbe'otekha*, אֶצְבְּעֹתֶ֑יךָ) in verse 4 to "hands" (*yadekha*, יָדֶ֑יךָ) in verse 7. Fingers suggest delicate artisanship—the heavens as filigree work. Hands connote power and ownership—creation as God's possession now entrusted to humanity. This lexical shift underscores the psalm's movement from contemplation to stewardship. The connection to Psalm 144 is instructive: there, a royal psalm, God "trains my hands (*yaday*) for battle, my fingers (*etzbe'otay*) for war" (144:1). In both psalms, hands and fingers mark degrees of divine involvement, but Psalm 8 inverts the military context—humanity's "battle" is not against enemies but for creation's flourishing.

The psalm's closing *inclusio* returns to the opening praise but with a crucial difference: it omits the line about God's splendor covering the heavens. Having contemplated creation and humanity's place within it, the psalm needs no longer assert divine transcendence. The journey from cosmic vastness through human smallness to delegated dominion has demonstrated God's majesty more powerfully than any declaration. The liturgical tradition understood this circular structure deeply: Psalm 8 appears in the High Holiday liturgy (*Yom Kippur Katan*) and its phrases permeate Jewish prayer. The verse "תַּ֭מְשִׁילֵהוּ בְּמַעֲשֵׂ֣י יָדֶ֑יךָ" ("You made him master over Your handiwork," v. 7) echoes in multiple contexts—from *Alenu* (where "בְּמַעֲשֵׂ֣י יָדֶ֑יךָ" recalls creation theology) to *Avinu Malkenu* on Yom Kippur. The phrase "מָה־אַדִּ֣יר שִׁ֭מְךָ בְּכׇל־הָאָ֑רֶץ" ("How majestic is Your name throughout the earth") was incorporated into *Ashrei* recitations and High Holiday Musaf services, where "מָה־אַדִּ֣יר שִׁ֭מְךָ" frames meditations on divine sovereignty and human responsibility.

Psalm 8 earned its canonical place not through theological novelty—Genesis 1 had already established humanity's divine image—but through poetic synthesis. It takes Genesis's prose declaration and refashions it as hymnic wonder, making the abstract ("image of God") viscerally concrete (crowned, enthroned, feet trampling creation). It addresses the perennial human oscillation between insignificance (under infinite stars) and grandeur (as meaning-makers and stewards). Most remarkably, it resolves this tension not through philosophical argument but through liturgical structure: the *inclusio* of praise transforms cosmological awe into covenantal intimacy. "יְהֹוָ֤ה אֲדֹנֵ֗ינוּ" ("LORD, our Lord")—the possessive "our" bridges transcendence and immanence, the cosmic and the covenantal. We are dust animated by divine breath, infants whose babble becomes fortress, creatures crowned with glory. That is the psalm's enduring paradox: humanity's true dignity lies precisely in acknowledging its contingency, its utter dependence on the gracious divine gaze that remembers, takes note, and crowns with majesty.

## Modern Jewish Liturgical Use

Psalm 8 is recited in its entirety during *Yom Kippur Katan* (the minor Day of Atonement observed on the eve of each new month), specifically in the *Seder Yom Kippur Katan* service. This placement connects the psalm's creation theology to monthly renewal and repentance, framing human dignity and dominion as gifts requiring responsible stewardship.

Individual phrases from the psalm permeate Jewish liturgy across multiple traditions and occasions. The psalm's opening "לַמְנַצֵּ֥חַ עַֽל־הַגִּתִּ֗ית" ("For the conductor, upon the *gittit*") appears in seven prayer contexts, including *Avinu Malkenu* (Ashkenaz), *Korbanot* sections, and daily *Song of the Day* recitations, primarily on weekdays, Shabbat, and Rosh Chodesh.

Most significantly, verse 7's "בְּמַעֲשֵׂ֣י יָדֶ֑יךָ" ("the work of Your hands") appears in 23 distinct liturgical contexts spanning Shabbat and Yom Kippur services, including *Alenu*, the *Amidah*, *Avinu Malkenu*, and the *Hodu* section of morning prayers. In these prayers, the phrase typically follows the pattern: "כִּי֩ בְרִיָּתֶ֨ךָ זֶ֤ה וַיְשִׁיבֵ֙נוּ֙ לְ֣תִמְכֵ֔ם וּלְרַחֵ֖ם בְּמַעֲשֵׂ֥ה יָדֶֽיךָ" ("for these are Your creatures... have mercy on Your handiwork"), transforming the psalm's statement of human dominion into a petition for divine compassion toward all creation.

The refrain "מָה־אַדִּ֣יר שִׁ֭מְךָ בְּכׇל־הָאָ֑רֶץ" ("How majestic is Your name throughout the earth") appears in High Holiday liturgy, particularly in *Kedushah* variations and the *Priestly Blessing* (*Birkat Kohanim*) during Yom Kippur's Musaf and Neilah services. The liturgical compilers understood this psalm's *inclusio* structure as a model for framing human acknowledgment of divine sovereignty—beginning

### VERSE-BY-VERSE COMMENTARY (for review)
# Verse-by-Verse Commentary: Psalm 8

---

**Verse 1**

לַמְנַצֵּ֥חַ עַֽל־הַגִּתִּ֗ית; מִזְמ֥וֹר לְדָוִֽד׃

This superscription contains one of the Hebrew Bible's most intriguing musical-liturgical enigmas: הַגִּתִּית (*haggittit*). The term appears only three times—here and in Psalms 81 and 84—yet its precise meaning remains contested after millennia of interpretation. The ancient versions diverge spectacularly in their renderings, revealing how early translators already struggled with this rare term. The Septuagint's "ὑπὲρ τῶν ληνῶν" ("concerning the winepresses") suggests a harvest or festival context, perhaps reading the word as related to גַּת (*gat*, "winepress"). This interpretation could connect the psalm to agricultural celebration, specifically Sukkot, when the grape harvest would be completed.

Medieval Jewish commentators offered competing theories. Rashi, drawing on Menachem ben Saruq's dictionary (*Machbereth*), suggested it referred to "a musical instrument that came from Gath" (כְּלִי זֶמֶר שֶׁבָּא מִגַּת), where skilled craftsmen produced it. This etymology finds the term's root in the Philistine city of Gath. Ibn Ezra, characteristically rationalist, proposed it designated "a kind of music" (מִמִּינֵי הַנִּגּוּן), a specific musical mode or melody type, though he also acknowledged the theory that David composed it during his sojourn in Gath (1 Sam 21:11-16). Radak synthesized these views, accepting it as "a kind of music" while noting the Gath connection. The Meiri took yet another tack, suggesting it referred to the Ark itself, which temporarily resided in "the house of Obed-edom the Gittite" (2 Sam 6:10-12), making הַגִּתִּית an oblique reference to that sacred object.

The term לַמְנַצֵּ֥חַ (*lamnatzeach*, "for the conductor/chief musician") appears in 55 psalm superscriptions, suggesting institutional worship leadership. The root נצח can mean "to be preeminent, to endure," linking the psalm to permanent liturgical repertoire. What unites Psalms 8, 81, and 84 beyond this rare designation? All three celebrate divine sovereignty over creation and Israel's unique relationship with that cosmic King, suggesting הַגִּתִּית may have indicated a specific liturgical occasion—perhaps the autumn festival cycle—when these themes took center stage.

---

**Verse 2**

יְהֹוָ֤ה אֲדֹנֵ֗ינוּ, מָה־אַדִּ֣יר שִׁ֭מְךָ בְּכׇל־הָאָ֑רֶץ; אֲשֶׁ֥ר תְּנָ֥ה ה֝וֹדְךָ֗ עַל־הַשָּׁמָֽיִם׃

The double divine name—יְהֹוָה אֲדֹנֵינוּ (*YHWH adonenu*, "YHWH our Lord")—creates an immediate intimacy wrapped in transcendence. The covenant name YHWH evokes Israel's particular relationship with God, while the possessive suffix "our Lord" (אֲדֹנֵינוּ) transforms cosmic majesty into personal belonging. This combination appears elsewhere (Ps 135:5, Is 26:13) but with special force here, framing the psalm's central paradox: a God whose "name" (שֵׁם, *shem*) fills all earth yet deigns to notice frail humanity.

The exclamation מָה־אַדִּיר (*mah-addir*, "How majestic!") employs the interrogative מָה not to question but to express wonderment beyond articulation—a rhetorical device common in hymnic praise (cf. Ps 31:20, מָה־רַב־טוּבְךָ, "How abundant is Your goodness!"). The adjective אַדִּיר (*addir*) carries connotations of nobility, might, and majesty; it describes both YHWH (Ex 15:6, 11, נֶאְדָּר בַּכֹּחַ, "majestic in power") and human nobility (Judg 5:13, אַדִּירִים, "nobles"). The phrase בְּכׇל־הָאָ֑רֶץ (*bekhol-ha'aretz*, "in all the earth") establishes universal scope—this is not tribal theology but cosmic acclamation.

The verse's second colon presents a notorious crux: אֲשֶׁ֥ר תְּנָה֥ הוֹדְךָ֗ עַל־הַשָּׁמָֽיִם (*asher tenah hodkha al-hashamayim*). The verb תְּנָה (*tenah*) has sparked centuries of debate. Is it imperative ("Give!") or perfect ("You have set")? The grammatical form could be either. Ibn Ezra understood it as "You have set Your majesty upon the heavens," emphasizing divine transcendence. Radak explained it as an infinitive absolute functioning as a finite verb: נָתַתָּ (*natata*, "You have set/placed"). The Malbim, characteristically, saw deeper significance: the divine הוֹד (*hod*, "splendor/majesty") primarily manifests on earth through creation, while in the heavens it exists in pure, unmediated form—thus the verse moves from earthly manifestation (בְּכׇל־הָאָ֑רֶץ) to heavenly essence (עַל־הַשָּׁמָֽיִם).

This liturgical opening appears prominently in Jewish worship. The phrase מָה־אַדִּ֣יר שִׁ֭מְךָ became woven into High Holiday liturgy, particularly in Neilah and Musaf services, where it punctuates confessions of divine sovereignty. The threefold iteration—יְהֹוָה אֲדֹנֵינוּ מָה־אַדִּיר שִׁמְךָ בְּכׇל־הָאָרֶץ—appears in Hoshana Rabba rituals and Kedushah expansions, suggesting the tradition read this verse as paradigmatic language for acknowledging God's cosmic kingship. The doubled divine name and the inclusive "our" make this not individual piety but communal acclamation, ideal for corporate worship contexts where Israel collectively affirms YHWH's universal reign.

---

**Verse 3**

מִפִּ֤י עוֹלְלִ֨ים וְֽיֹנְקִים֮, יִסַּ֢דְתָּ֫ עֹ֥ז; לְמַ֥עַן צוֹרְרֶ֑יךָ, לְהַשְׁבִּ֥ית א֝וֹיֵ֗ב וּמִתְנַקֵּֽם׃

This verse executes a jarring tonal shift from cosmic grandeur to infant babble. The phrase מִפִּ֤י עוֹלְלִ֨ים וְֽיֹנְקִים (*mipi ololim veyonqim*, "from the mouth of babes and sucklings") introduces radical vulnerability as divine instrument. The noun עוֹלְלִ֨ים (*ololim*) derives from a root associated with immaturity and dependency, while יֹנְקִים (*yonqim*, "those who suck") specifies nursing infants. Rashi, connecting עוֹלְלִים to Job 16:15's וְעוֹלַלְתִּי בֶעָפָר קַרְנִי ("and I have laid my glory in the dust"), understood the term as emphasizing human lowliness: even the Levites and priests who offer praise are "people raised in filth, like babes and sucklings" (בְּנֵי אָדָם גְּדֵלִים בְּלִכְלוּךְ, כְּעוֹלְלִים וְיוֹנְקֵי שָׁדַיִם).

The verb יִסַּדְתָּ (*yissadta*, "You have founded/established") employs architectural metaphor—God lays a foundation (יְסוֹד, *yesod*) for עֹז (*oz*). But what is this עֹז that emerges from infant lips? The term means "strength, might, power" (cf. Ps 29:11, יְהֹוָה עֹז לְעַמּוֹ יִתֵּן, "YHWH will give strength to His people"). Some translate it "praise" following the Septuagint's αἶνον (*ainon*), but BDB confirms its primary meaning as "strength, fortress, stronghold." The paradox intensifies: God establishes military-grade defense (עֹז as fortress) from the weakest possible source. The Talmud (Shabbat 88b) interpreted this messianically—the angels' objection to giving Torah to flesh-and-blood humanity was answered by citing this verse: even human fragility (עוֹלְלִים וְיֹנְקִים) can become divine strength.

The verse's second half introduces martial language: לְמַ֥עַן צוֹרְרֶ֑יךָ לְהַשְׁבִּ֥ית א֝וֹיֵ֗ב וּמִתְנַקֵּֽם (*lema'an tzorerekha lehashbit oyev umitnaqem*, "on account of Your foes, to silence enemy and avenger"). Who are these cosmic adversaries in a creation psalm? The vocabulary—צֹרֵר (*tzorer*, "adversary"), אוֹיֵב (*oyev*, "enemy"), מִתְנַקֵּם (*mitnaqem*, "avenger")—typically describes military opponents but here suggests ideological or cosmic forces opposing divine sovereignty. This may preserve traces of Ancient Near Eastern combat mythology, where creation involves subduing chaos powers. Yet the psalmist has sublimated such imagery: the divine warrior doesn't wield lightning and storm but infant praise. The verb הַשְׁבִּית (*hashbit*, "to cause to cease, to silence") in Hiphil suggests not merely defeat but permanent cessation of opposition.

Matthew 21:16 famously appropriates this verse when Jesus, hearing children cry הוֹשַׁעְנָא in the Temple, silences critics by quoting: "Have you never read, 'Out of the mouth of infants and nursing babies you have prepared praise'?" The New Testament reading interprets עֹז as "praise," making infant acclaim paradigmatic worship. But the Hebrew's martial imagery—founding strength to silence enemies—suggests something more radical: God's power manifests precisely through what the world considers weak, making the powerless the locus of divine might.

This verse entered liturgical tradition in the Kedushah expansions and Reader's Repetition, particularly in Edot HaMizrach rites, where מִפִּי עוֹלְלִים introduces themes of humble praise overwhelming proud opposition—a theological claim about whose worship ultimately prevails.

---

**Verse 4**

כִּֽי־אֶרְאֶ֣ה שָׁ֭מֶיךָ, מַעֲשֵׂ֣ה אֶצְבְּעֹתֶ֑יךָ; יָרֵ֥חַ וְ֝כוֹכָבִ֗ים, אֲשֶׁ֣ר כּוֹנָֽנְתָּה׃

The causal כִּי (*ki*, "when/because") pivots from infant praise to personal contemplation. The verb אֶרְאֶ֣ה (*er'eh*, "I behold") shifts the psalm from third-person theology to first-person experience—this is phenomenology, the poet gazing upward. The phrase מַעֲשֵׂ֣ה אֶצְבְּעֹתֶ֑יךָ (*ma'aseh etzbe'otekha*, "work of Your fingers") introduces one of Scripture's most delicate divine images. Why "fingers" rather than "hands"? The term אֶצְבַּע (*etzba*, "finger") connotes precision, artisanal craft, detailed workmanship. When Exodus 8:19 reports the Egyptian magicians declaring אֶצְבַּע אֱלֹהִים הִוא ("This is the finger of God!"), they recognize a precision beyond human replication. When Exodus 31:18 describes the tablets as כְּתֻבִים בְּאֶצְבַּע אֱלֹהִים ("written by the finger of God"), it emphasizes divine craftsmanship, not brute power.

Yet verse 7 will shift to יָדֶיךָ (*yadekha*, "Your hands")—a deliberate lexical contrast. Ibn Ezra noted this: fingers create the vast heavens with delicate artistry; hands govern earthly creation that humanity rules. The progression suggests hierarchy: the celestial realm displays God's finest filigree work, while terrestrial creatures fall under the governance of God's "hands," which humanity then administers. This is not arbitrary word variation but a calculated architectural metaphor about scaled divine involvement.

The verse specifies only יָרֵ֥חַ וְכוֹכָבִים (*yare'ach vekokhavim*, "moon and stars"), conspicuously omitting the sun. Ibn Ezra and Radak both noted this: the psalm reflects nocturnal contemplation. "By day," Radak explained, "only the sun is seen, but at night a very great host is seen" (ביום לא יראה כי אם השמש לבדו, ובלילה יראה צבא גדול מאוד). The moon (יָרֵחַ, *yare'ach*) and stars (כּוֹכָבִים, *kokhavim*) visible against darkness invite wonder more than the overwhelming solar glare. This reading finds support in Genesis 1:16's subordination of sun and moon to "lights" (מְאֹרֹת) rather than treating them as deities—a deliberate anti-mythological move. Here too the psalmist diminutive-izes celestial bodies: they are merely "work of fingers," not gods to worship.

The verb כּוֹנָֽנְתָּה (*konanta*, "You established/set in place") comes from כּוּן (*kun*, "to be firm, to establish"), suggesting both fixity and ordering. The Piel form indicates intensive action: God didn't merely place but firmly established, arranged, ordered. This cosmic "setting in place" echoes Creation language (cf. Ps 89:3, שָׁמַיִם תָּכִן אֱמוּנָתְךָ בָהֶם, "the heavens—You establish Your faithfulness in them"). Jeremiah 10:12 uses the same root: מֵכִין תֵּבֵל בְּחָכְמָתוֹ ("He established the world by His wisdom"), linking כּוּן to divine wisdom's architectural role. Against Ancient Near Eastern cosmogonies where gods fashion cosmos from defeated enemies' corpses (Enuma Elish), the psalmist offers finger-craftsmanship and careful establishment—artisan, not warrior; wisdom, not violence.

This verse's phrase appears in Blessing of the Moon liturgies (Kiddush Levanah), where witnessing the monthly renewal prompts blessing: יוֹצֵר בְּרֵאשִׁית... וְלַלְּבָנָה אָמַר שֶׁתִּתְחַדֵּשׁ ("Creator of beginnings... and to the moon He said that it should renew itself")—the psalmist's nocturnal contemplation of יָרֵחַ becomes ritual occasion for acknowledging ongoing divine governance.

---

**Verse 5**

מָה־אֱנ֥וֹשׁ, כִּֽי־תִזְכְּרֶ֑נּוּ; וּבֶן־אָ֝דָ֗ם, כִּ֣י תִפְקְדֶֽנּוּ׃

The psalm's theological pivot arrives as rhetorical question. The interrogative מָה (*mah*, "what?") echoes verse 2's מָה־אַדִּיר ("How majestic!") but inverts its register—from cosmic majesty to human insignificance. The double parallelism employs synonymous Hebrew terms for humanity, each carrying distinct semantic freight. אֱנוֹשׁ (*enosh*) derives from a root associated with frailty, mortality, and weakness. BDB notes this term "emphasizes man's weakness and mortality" (cf. Job 4:17, הַאֱנוֹשׁ מֵאֱלוֹהַ יִצְדָּק, "Can mortal man be righteous before God?"). The second term, בֶּן־אָדָם (*ben-adam*, "son of man"), literalizes humanity's earthiness—אָדָם shares etymological roots with אֲדָמָה (*adamah*, "ground, earth"), thus ben-adam is "son of the soil," emphasizing creatureliness and terrestrial origin. This doublet intensifies the contrast: the finger-crafted heavens versus dust-derived humanity.

The verbs תִזְכְּרֶנּוּ (*tizkərenu*, "You remember him") and תִפְקְדֶנּוּ (*tifqədenu*, "You visit/attend to him") are not casual attention. זָכַר (*zakhar*, "to remember") in biblical Hebrew often carries covenantal significance—God "remembers" covenants (Gen 9:15, Noah; Ex 2:24, Exodus), meaning active engagement, not mental recall. Similarly, פָּקַד (*paqad*, "to attend, visit, muster") implies purposeful action, whether blessing or judgment. When God "visits" (פָּקַד) Sarah (Gen 21:1), she conceives; when God "visits" (פָּקַד) iniquity (Ex 32:34), punishment follows. Here both verbs denote divine attentiveness—not divine omniscience abstractly but God's active, caring mindfulness of humanity.

This verse became exegetically contested. Job 7:17-18 bitterly parodies it: מָה־אֱנוֹשׁ כִּי־תְגַדְּלֶנּוּ וְכִי־תָשִׁית אֵלָיו לִבֶּךָ וַתִּפְקְדֶנּוּ לִבְקָרִים ("What is man that You make him great, that You set Your heart on him, that You examine him every morning?"). For Job, divine attention becomes oppressive surveillance; for Psalm 8, it's sheer wonder and grace. The same vocabulary yields opposite theodicies—Job indicts God's excessive attention to human affairs, while Psalm 8 marvels at God's condescension. The intertextual debate reveals how Israel wrestled with the tension between divine transcendence and immanence, between cosmic sovereignty and intimate concern.

Medieval commentators recognized the verse's gravitational center. Radak explained: "When I consider... the great creations and intelligences [the celestial bodies], how much more so [the vastness of] man—that You remembered him and bestowed upon him something of the splendor of the higher intelligences that are not corporeal" (כשאראה... בריאות גדולות ומשכילות... מה אנוש כי זכרת אותו ואצלת עליו מהוד המשכילים העליונים שאינם גוף). For Radak, the question isn't merely rhetorical deflection but genuinely puzzling—why would the cosmic artisan notice ephemeral humanity? The answer lies in verses 6-9: humanity receives dignity not despite but through divine attention.

The Talmud (Shabbat 88b) dramatized this question through angelic protest. When God proposes creating humanity, ministering angels object: "Sovereign of the Universe, what are his deeds?" Shown humanity's future corruption—the generation of the Flood, the builders of Babel—they repeat Psalm 8:5: מָה־אֱנוֹשׁ כִּי־תִזְכְּרֶנּוּ ("What is man that You should remember him?"). God's response: עַד־זִקְנָה אֲנִי הוּא וְעַד־שֵׂיבָה אֲנִי אֶסְבֹּל (Is 46:4, "Even to old age I am He, even to gray hairs I will bear"). The aggadic reading makes Psalm 8:5 express genuine perplexity—not just human wonderment but cosmic befuddlement at divine grace persisting despite human failure. God's "remembering" becomes covenantal loyalty rather than logical assessment.

---

**Verse 6**

וַתְּחַסְּרֵ֣הוּ מְּ֭עַט מֵאֱלֹהִ֑ים; וְכָב֖וֹד וְהָדָ֣ר תְּעַטְּרֵֽהוּ׃

Here erupts Scripture's most contested anthropological claim. The verb וַתְּחַסְּרֵהוּ (*vatechasrehu*, "You made him lack/reduced him") comes from חָסֵר (*chaser*, "to lack, be diminished"), typically denoting deficiency. Yet the phrase מְּעַט מֵאֱלֹהִים (*me'at me'elohim*) transforms lack into proximity—"a little less than elohim." But who or what is אֱלֹהִים (*elohim*) here? The term's notorious polyvalence generates interpretive crisis. It can mean: (1) the one God; (2) gods (plural); (3) divine beings/angels; (4) human judges (Ex 22:8). The Septuagint opted decisively for "angels" (παρ' ἀγγέλους, *par' angelous*), a reading followed by Rashi, Ibn Ezra, Radak, and most medieval Jewish commentators.

Rashi explained this choice through biblical exemplars: "You gave power to Joshua to still the sun and dry up the Jordan, to Moses to split the Sea of Reeds and ascend to the heavens, to Elijah to resurrect the dead" (נתת כח ביהושע להדמים החמה וליבש את הירדן, במשה לקרוע מי ים סוף ולעלות למרום, באליהו להחיות את המת). These miraculous powers temporarily elevated humans to angelic-level capacities, demonstrating what מְּעַט מֵאֱלֹהִים means functionally. Ibn Ezra grounded this reading philosophically: the human נְשָׁמָה (*neshamah*, "spirit/soul") shares the incorporeal quality of angels (מִתְכֹּנֶת הַמַּלְאָכִים שֶׁאֵינָהּ גוּף), the "diminishment" being embodiment itself—the soul trapped in flesh.

Radak synthesized: "The spirit of man is of the same quality as the angels, which is incorporeal just as they are incorporeal; and the inferiority consists in its being lodged in a body" (נשמת האדם מתכונת המלאכים שאיננה גוף כמו שהם אינם גופות; והחסרון הוא לפי שהיא בגוף). For medieval Jewish rationalists, humanity's "nearness to elohim/angels" resides in the intellective soul, capable of abstract thought, moral reasoning, and comprehending divine law—capacities that distinguish humans from animals and approximate angelic nature.

Yet reading מֵאֱלֹהִים as "less than God" rather than "less than angels" remains grammatically legitimate and theologically provocative. If humans are "little lower than God Himself," the psalm makes an even more audacious claim about imago Dei—humanity as God's closest analogue in creation. This reading finds support in Genesis 1:26-27's "in our image, after our likeness" (בְּצַלְמֵנוּ כִּדְמוּתֵנוּ), where the first-person plural may indicate God addressing the heavenly court but results in humanity uniquely stamped with divine צֶלֶם (*tzelem*, "image"). Psalm 8:6 could thus interpret Genesis 1: humanity was made "a little lower than divine status" but compensated through coronation with כָּבוֹד וְהָדָר.

The dual nouns כָּבוֹד וְהָדָר (*kavod vehadar*, "glory and majesty") typically describe God Himself (Ps 96:6, הוֹד וְהָדָר לְפָנָיו, "splendor and majesty are before Him"; 104:1, הוֹד וְהָדָר לָבָשְׁתָּ, "You are clothed with glory and majesty"). Their application to humanity constitutes theological revolution—the democratization of royal-divine attributes. In Ancient Near Eastern ideology, only the king bore divine image and exercised cosmic dominion; Egyptian texts call Pharaoh "the image of Re," Mesopotamian inscriptions depict kings as divine agents subduing chaos. Psalm 8 explodes this monopoly: every human receives the corona, all humanity wears glory.

The verb תְּעַטְּרֵֽהוּ (*te'atrenu*, "You crown him") from עָטַר (*atar*, "to encircle, crown") appears only here and Psalm 65:12 (עִטַּרְתָּ שְׁנַת טוֹבָתֶךָ, "You crown the year with Your goodness"). The coronation metaphor transforms anthropology into royal ceremony—God as king-maker, humanity as crowned regent. This imagery entered Jewish mysticism: the Zohar interprets כָּבוֹד וְהָדָר as sefirot (divine emanations) humanity channels into material realm, making humans theourgic agents. The Malbim saw here humanity's quadruple causation: material (dust), formal (divine image), efficient (divine breath), and final (stewardship over creation). Each cause demonstrates humanity's unique position "between beasts and angels."

Hebrews 2:6-9 quotes this verse christologically, applying it to Jesus: "You made him for a little while lower than the angels" (βραχύ τι παρ' ἀγγέλους, *brachy ti par' angelous*), interpreting מְּעַט temporally ("for a little while") rather than quantitatively ("a little lower"). The New Testament reading makes incarnation the fulfillment of Psalm 8's anthropology—the divine becoming human to complete humanity's coronation. This exegetical move reveals how christology appropriated Jewish creation theology, though it departs from the psalm's plain sense, which speaks of humanity generically, not a particular messianic figure.

The phrase וְכָבוֹד וְהָדָר appears liturgically in Edot HaMizrach Shabbat evening songs, woven into zemirot celebrating Shabbat as return to Eden—the day when humanity's original kavod is restored through rest from labor.

---

**Verse 7**

תַּ֭מְשִׁילֵהוּ בְּמַעֲשֵׂ֣י יָדֶ֑יךָ; כֹּ֝֗ל שַׁ֣תָּה תַֽחַת־רַגְלָֽיו׃

The divine coronation (v. 6) immediately translates into terrestrial governance. The verb תַּמְשִׁילֵהוּ (*tamshilehu*, "You make him rule/have dominion") in Hiphil causative suggests God's active installation of humanity as viceroy—this is delegated authority, not autonomous power. The root מָשַׁל (*mashal*) means "to rule, govern, have dominion" and appears throughout royal contexts (Gen 1:18, וְלִמְשֹׁל בַּיּוֹם וּבַלַּיְלָה, "to rule over day and night," regarding sun and moon; Judg 8:22, מְשֹׁל־בָּנוּ, "rule over us," the people request of Gideon). Human governance here mirrors—in creaturely register—God's cosmic sovereignty.

The object of this governance is בְּמַעֲשֵׂ֣י יָדֶ֑יךָ (*bema'aseh yadekha*, "the works of Your hands"). Note the lexical shift from verse 4's מַעֲשֵׂ֣ה אֶצְבְּעֹתֶ֑יךָ ("work of Your fingers"). The heavens—displayed in finger-wrought artistry—remain beyond human jurisdiction. But earthly creation falls under יָדֶיךָ (*yadekha*, "Your hands"), which humanity administers. Ibn Ezra noted this precisely: "The works of Your hands—on earth alone, as he will mention them afterward" (במעשי ידיך בארץ לבדה כאשר יזכירם אחר כן). The shift from fingers (delicate craftsmanship) to hands (power, ownership) marks descending scalar involvement—God's fine-detail work versus His gross motor governance, which He entrusts to humanity.

This phrase בְּמַעֲשֵׂ֣י יָדֶ֑יךָ became one of Scripture's most liturgically saturated. It appears in twenty-three distinct prayer contexts spanning Shabbat and Yom Kippur services—Alenu, Amidah, Avinu Malkenu, Hodu, Musaf, Neilah. In these liturgies, the phrase typically triggers petitions for divine mercy: כִּי בְרִיּוֹתֶיךָ אֵלֶּה, רַחֵם עַל מַעֲשֵׂה יָדֶיךָ ("for these are Your creatures... have mercy on Your handiwork"). The liturgical reversal is striking: the psalm's statement of human dominion becomes a plea for divine compassion. Corporate worship recognizes that if we are "masters over Your handiwork," we ourselves remain handiwork requiring mercy. The phrase thus oscillates between empowerment and dependence, dignity and neediness.

The second colon's imagery intensifies royal-conquest connotations: כֹּ֝ל שַׁתָּה תַֽחַת־רַגְלָֽיו (*kol shattah tachat raglav*, "all things You have placed under his feet"). The verb שָׁתָה (*shattah*, Qal perfect 2ms from שִׁית, "to set, place") indicates deliberate positioning. תַֽחַת־רַגְלָֽיו (*tachat raglav*, "under his feet") employs Ancient Near Eastern royal iconography where conquered enemies prostrate beneath the victor's feet. Egyptian reliefs depict Pharaoh treading on foreign captives; Mesopotamian victory stelae show kings with foot atop vanquished foes. Yet Psalm 8 sublimates this violence—the entities "under foot" aren't defeated nations but creation itself, positioned for human stewardship, not destruction.

Joshua 10:24 literalizes this image: when Israel's commanders place feet on captured Canaanite kings' necks, Joshua declares: כָּכָה יַעֲשֶׂה יְהוָה לְכָל־אֹיְבֵיכֶם ("Thus will YHWH do to all your enemies"). But Psalm 8's "under foot" lacks martial violence—creation submits not through defeat but through creational order. The footstool metaphor appears in 1 Chronicles 28:2 (הֲדֹם רַגְלֵי אֱלֹהֵינוּ, "footstool of our God"), referring to the Ark, and Lamentations 2:1 (הֲדֹם רַגְלָיו, "His footstool"), regarding Zion. In Psalm 110:1, YHWH says to David's lord, עַד־אָשִׁית אֹיְבֶיךָ הֲדֹם לְרַגְלֶיךָ ("until I make your enemies your footstool")—explicitly martial. Psalm 8 borrows this regal language but redirects it: humanity's "enemies" aren't political foes but chaos potentially latent in untended creation.

The phrase תַֽחַת־רַגְלָֽיו also appears in Customs of Yom Kippur Night (Ashkenaz), woven into confessional liturgy—humanity's "dominion" becomes occasion for acknowledging how we've misused delegated authority, turning stewardship into exploitation. This liturgical recontextualization reads Genesis 1:28's "subdue" (כִּבְשֻׁהָ) and Psalm 8:7's "under his feet" through the lens of ecological ethics—dominion entails responsibility, the corona obligates care.

---

**Verse 8**

צֹנֶ֣ה וַאֲלָפִ֣ים כֻּלָּ֑ם; וְ֝גַ֗ם בַּהֲמ֥וֹת שָׂדָֽי׃

The dominion catalog begins with domesticated creatures nearest human habitation. The noun צֹנֶה (*tzoneh*) presents textual-linguistic interest: it's a rare form for צֹאן (*tzo'n*, "flock"). Rashi explained: "צנה is an expression of industry/labor [literally: 'making'], as in 'and enclosures for your flocks' (Num 32:24, וּגְדֵרֹת לְצֹנַאֲכֶם)" (צנה לשון פעולה, כמו וגדרת לצנאכם). The term emphasizes not just animals but their management—penned flocks requiring human tending. The noun אֲלָפִים (*alafim*) means "cattle, oxen" (cf. Deut 7:13, וְעַשְׁתְּרֹת צֹאנֶךָ שְׁגַר אֲלָפֶיךָ, "the increase of your flock, the young of your cattle"). These are the animals of agricultural life—sheep for wool and meat, cattle for labor and dairy.

The emphatic כֻּלָּ֑ם (*kulam*, "all of them") reinforces totality: every domesticated beast falls under human governance. Then comes the expansion marker וְגַם (*vegam*, "and also"), introducing בַּהֲמ֥וֹת שָׂדָֽי (*bahemot saday*, "beasts of the field")—the wild creatures beyond farmyard and pasture. The term בְּהֵמָה (*behemah*) can refer generically to animals (Gen 1:24) but here, preceded by domesticated צֹנֶה וַאֲלָפִים, it signifies non-domesticated fauna. The construct שָׂדָֽי (*saday*, "of field") specifies their habitat—open territory where human control is less direct.

Ibn Ezra clarified the catalog's logic: "He mentioned sheep and cattle because of his need for them for food and clothing more than for riding horses and donkeys. Then he mentioned wild beasts" (הזכיר הצאן והאלפים, בעבור צרכו למאכלו ומלבושו יותר מרכיבת הסוסים והחמורים, ואחר כן הזכיר חיות השדה). The progression moves from essential sustenance animals to working animals (horses, donkeys—implicitly included in "all") to wildlife. This ordering reflects anthropocentric perspective: creation radiates outward from human needs.

Radak added: "Flocks (צנה) are sheep and goats; cattle (אלפים) are oxen. And he said 'all of them' regarding the other beasts that grow up with him—horses, donkeys, camels. And he said 'and also beasts of the field,' who are the wild animals... even those who don't grow up with him in settlements, over them he rules by his intellect and cunning" (צנה הם הכבשים והעזים. אלפים הם השורים. ואמר כלם, על שאר הבהמות הגדלות עמו—הסוסים והחמורים והגמלים. ואמר וגם בהמות שדי, שהם חיות השדה... אותם שאינם גדלים עמו בישוב, בהם הוא מושל בשכלו ובתחבולותיו). Human governance extends even to creatures beyond domestication through intellect, strategy, trapping, and hunting—a realistic acknowledgment that "dominion" doesn't mean all animals serve humanity directly but that human cunning can encompass even the wild.

This verse's phrase כֻּלָּם appears liturgically in the Zikhronot section of Rosh Hashanah Musaf and Amidah contexts, typically in constructions like כָּל־מַעֲשֶׂיךָ ("all Your works"), expanding the psalm's animal catalog to encompass total creation remembering its Creator. The liturgical move universalizes the psalm's anthropocentric focus—if humans govern creation (v. 7-9), all creation participates in covenantal relationship with God, making dominion not exploitation but stewardship within sacred ecology.

Genesis 1:26's "Let them have dominion over the fish of the sea, the birds of the heavens, and over the cattle (בַּבְּהֵמָה), and over all the earth, and over every creeping thing" provides the obvious intertext. But Psalm 8's ordering differs subtly: Genesis proceeds sea-sky-land (fish, birds, cattle, earth, creepers), while Psalm 8 starts land-based (sheep, cattle, wild beasts), then moves to sky (birds, v. 9), finally sea (fish, v. 9). This may reflect phenomenological experience—the poet catalogs what's accessible to observation, moving from most familiar (livestock) to most remote (sea creatures).

The phrase also resonates with Noah narrative, where animals enter the ark צֹאן וּבָקָר (Gen 7:23, "flocks and cattle"), suggesting preservation of domestic species ensures civilization's continuity. Humanity's dominion includes responsibility for creaturely survival—a point ecological ethics can mine from this verse.

---

**Verse 9**

צִפּ֣וֹר שָׁ֭מַיִם וּדְגֵ֣י הַיָּ֑ם; עֹ֝בֵ֗ר אׇרְח֥וֹת יַמִּֽים׃

The dominion catalog ascends to sky and descends to sea, completing creation's triple-register (land-sky-sea). The phrase צִפּוֹר שָׁמַיִם (*tzipor shamayim*, "birds of the heavens") employs the standard biblical designation for avifauna (cf. Gen 1:26, 28; Deut 4:17). The genitive שָׁמַיִם (*shamayim*, "heavens") indicates habitat—these creatures occupy airspace, the realm inaccessible to terrestrial humanity except through observation and imagination. Despite flying far above, they too fall under human governance, whether through domestication (fowl), hunting, or ecosystem management.

The phrase וּדְגֵ֣י הַיָּ֑ם (*udeghei hayam*, "and fish of the sea") completes the cosmic triad. The term דָּג (*dag*, "fish") appears frequently in Genesis 1's creation account and prophetic texts (Ezek 29:4-5, fish as metaphor for Egyptian masses). The sea (יָם, *yam*) represents in biblical cosmology the boundary of human habitation—mysterious, dangerous, chaotic. Yet even sea-dwellers, most remote from terrestrial humanity, are placed "under foot."

Then comes the psalm's poetic flourish: עֹבֵר אׇרְחוֹת יַמִּים (*over orḥot yammim*, "whatever passes through the paths of the seas"). This phrase exceeds the catalog's utility—it adds no new category but elaborates poetically on sea creatures. The term אֹרַח (*orah*, "path, way") typically describes land routes (Gen 18:11, אֹרַח כַּנָּשִׁים, "the way of women"; Job 16:22, אֹרַח לֹא־אָשׁוּב, "path whence I shall not return"). Applied to seas, it's striking—אׇרְחוֹת יַמִּים suggests navigable routes through chaotic waters, order imposed on formlessness, trackways within the trackless.

Ibn Ezra and Radak both understood עֹבֵר as referring to humanity itself: "And he mentioned this because [humans] catch fish in the heart of the sea" (והזכיר זה, בעבור שיצוד הדגים בלב הים, Ibn Ezra). "And further, by his intellect and cunning devices, [man] passes over the paths of the seas, for he makes ships and passes in them over the paths of the sea" (ועוד בשכלו ובתחבולותיו עבר ארחות ימים, שעושה ספינות ועובר בהם ארחות ימים, Radak). This reading makes עֹבֵר humanity's seafaring technology—shipbuilding, navigation, fishing. Humanity "traverses sea-paths," demonstrating dominion even in the realm most alien to terrestrial mammals.

But the phrase can also describe sea creatures themselves—"whatever traverses sea-paths," i.e., marine animals following migratory routes or ocean currents. Modern readers might see prescience here: ocean creatures do follow specific paths (whale migrations, salmon runs, tuna highways). The phrase's ambiguity enriches meaning—it can refer to human navigation or animal migration, and the poet may intend both: sea creatures travel their appointed courses, while humans sail these same waters, both participating in maritime order.

The phrase "paths of the seas" entered Jewish liturgical language through various Hodu and Hallel contexts, particularly Psalm 136 recitations where God's mastery over creation (הוֹלֵךְ עַמּוֹ בַמִּדְבָּר, "who led His people through the wilderness") includes sea-splitting (גֹּזֵר יַם־סוּף לִגְזָרִים, "who divided the Sea of Reeds into parts"). The phrase evokes God's imposition of structure on watery chaos, echoing Genesis 1:2's תְהוֹם (*tehom*, "deep") and the Spirit hovering עַל־פְּנֵי הַמָּיִם ("over the face of the waters").

This verse also resonates with modern oceanography's discovery of actual ocean "paths"—currents like the Gulf Stream—validating the psalmist's poetic intuition. Matthew Fontaine Maury, 19th-century American oceanographer, credited Psalm 8:9 with inspiring his research into ocean currents, claiming the phrase "paths of the seas" suggested such routes must exist. Whether this is hagiographic legend or historical fact, it demonstrates how Scripture's metaphors can stimulate scientific imagination.

The dominion catalog (vv. 8-9) deliberately echoes Genesis 1:26-28's creation mandate: וְיִרְדּוּ בִדְגַת הַיָּם וּבְעוֹף הַשָּׁמַיִם ("and let them have dominion over the fish of the sea and over the birds of the heavens"). But Psalm 8 adds agricultural specificity (sheep, cattle) and that haunting phrase "paths of the seas," transforming prose mandate into poetic wonder. The repetition signals canonical dialogue—later biblical texts meditating on Torah's foundational claims, exploring their implications through fresh imagery and emphasis.

---

**Verse 10**

יְהֹוָ֥ה אֲדֹנֵ֑ינוּ, מָה־אַדִּ֥יר שִׁ֝מְךָ֗ בְּכׇל־הָאָֽרֶץ׃

The psalm closes by returning to its opening formula—יְהֹוָה אֲדֹנֵינוּ מָה־אַדִּיר שִׁמְךָ בְּכׇל־הָאָרֶץ (*YHWH adonenu mah-addir shimkha bekhol-ha'aretz*, "YHWH our Lord, how majestic is Your name in all the earth!")—creating a perfect inclusio or envelope structure. This rhetorical device (Hebrew: מסגרת, *misgeret*, "frame") brackets the psalm's theological content with identical praise, guiding interpretation. The repetition signals: everything between these boundaries—from infant praise (v. 3) through nocturnal contemplation (v. 4), anthropological question (v. 5), divine coronation (v. 6), and dominion catalog (vv. 7-9)—amplifies the opening's wonderment at divine majesty manifest in creation and humanity's place within it.

Yet note the crucial omission: verse 2 included אֲשֶׁר תְּנָה הוֹדְךָ עַל־הַשָּׁמָיִם ("who sets Your majesty upon the heavens"), but verse 10 stops at בְּכׇל־הָאָרֶץ ("in all the earth"). The heavenly clause disappears. Why? Radak explained: "He returns to praise again when he has finished recounting the lovingkindness [God] shows toward man and the power He has given him over all" (שב עוד להודות כאשר כלה לספר החסד שעשה לאדם והיכלת שנתן לו על הכל). Having explored humanity's cosmic significance (vv. 4-9), the psalmist needs no longer assert divine transcendence abstractly—the journey through creation and anthropology has demonstrated God's majesty more powerfully than any declaration. The shortened closing allows the psalm's theological content to speak for itself.

This structural choice reveals sophisticated artistry. The opening inclusio establishes divine transcendence and earthly manifestation; the closing inclusio focuses solely on earthly manifestation, suggesting that understanding creation and humanity's role within it constitutes the proper path to apprehending divine glory. The progression is pedagogical: verse 2's claim about heavens becomes unnecessary after the contemplative journey—we now see God's majesty *through* creation rather than needing to be told about it abstractly.

The liturgical tradition deeply embedded this verse's opening phrase. It appears fourteen times across multiple prayer contexts—Amidah (Edot HaMizrach), Arvit for Motzei Rosh Hashana, Ashrei, Ashrei Uva L'Tziyon, Kedushah variations, Priestly Blessing, Yom Kippur Musaf and Neilah services. Particularly striking is its use in Yom Kippur liturgy, where יְהֹוָה אֲדֹנֵינוּ מָה־אַדִּיר שִׁמְךָ punctuates confessions of divine sovereignty at the liturgical year's apex. The tradition understands this verse as paradigmatic language for acknowledging God's cosmic kingship—not philosophical abstraction but experiential wonder grounded in creation's observable order.

The phrase also appears in Hoshana Rabba rituals (Sefard and Ashkenaz), where it frames the final day of Sukkot's supplications. The connection to harvest festival resonates with the verse's creation theology—just as Psalm 8 contemplates God's governance through natural order (moon, stars, animals, seas), Sukkot celebrates agricultural bounty as divine gift. The liturgical placement makes cosmic theology concrete: we acknowledge God's "majestic name in all the earth" precisely through agricultural thanksgiving, recognizing that dominion over creation (vv. 7-9) entails gratitude for creation's sustaining power.

The inclusio structure also invites comparison with other envelope-structured psalms. Psalm 103 begins and ends with בָּרְכִי נַפְשִׁי אֶת־יְהוָה ("Bless YHWH, O my soul"); Psalm 118 with הוֹדוּ לַיהוָה כִּי־טוֹב ("Give thanks to YHWH, for He is good"). These frames create liturgical unity but also theological emphasis—the framing phrase encapsulates the psalm's core message. For Psalm 8, that core is: divine majesty manifests specifically and paradoxically through creation's order and humanity's dignified-yet-dependent place within it. The psalm teaches not divine transcendence in isolation but transcendence-made-manifest in immanence, cosmic sovereignty observable in creaturely detail.

Medieval Jewish commentators noted how the closing verse's simplicity masks profundity. The Malbim argued that the inclusio's repetition with variation demonstrates dialectical movement: the opening states divine majesty as premise; the body explores its implications through creation and humanity; the closing returns to divine majesty now enriched by that exploration—same words, deeper meaning. This hermeneutical move (common in Malbim's method) treats repetition not as mechanical closure but as transformed restatement, the closing phrase carrying semantic freight accumulated through the psalm's journey.

Christian interpretation, particularly Hebrews 2:5-9's appropriation of verses 5-7, read Psalm 8 christologically—Jesus as the "son of man" crowned with "glory and honor." Yet the psalm's inclusio resists narrow messianic reading; the frame's universal scope ("in all the earth") and the dominion catalog's generic humanity ("You make him master...") indicate the psalmist contemplates humanity as such, not a particular salvific figure. The psalm's reception history thus reveals interpretive tensions: Jewish tradition read it as creation theology and anthropology; Christian tradition as christological prophecy. The text's ambiguity—especially verse 5's מָה־אֱנוֹשׁ... בֶן־אָדָם—enabled both readings, though the psalm's literary structure (inclusio framing generic dominion catalog) supports the former.

In sum, verse 10's return to verse 2's opening executes several rhetorical functions simultaneously: (1) structural closure through inclusio; (2) theological emphasis on divine majesty in creation; (3) transformation through repetition—same words, enriched meaning; (4) liturgical utility as paradigmatic divine-sovereignty formula. The verse's elegant simplicity conceals sophisticated artistry: by stripping away verse 2's heaven-clause, it makes the entire psalm's content the demonstration of what verse 2 asserted—God's name is majestic in all the earth precisely because of creation's ordered beauty and humanity's paradoxical dignity-within-dependence. The closing verse doesn't merely repeat but recapitulates, distilling the psalm's theological journey into renewed wonderment at divine sovereignty manifest in the created order we've just contemplated.

### עֹז
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: עֹז  

and (rarely) עוֹז n.m. strength, might;—abs. ע׳ Ju 9:51 +, עוֹז ψ 84:6 +, עָז־ Is 26:1, once עָ֑ז Gn 49:3; cstr. עֹז Mi 5:3 +, עָז־ ψ 28:8; sf. עָזִּי Ex 15:2 + 2 times, עֻזִּי ψ 28:7 +; עָזְּךָ 21:2 + 2 times, עֻזְּךָ 66:3 +; sf. 1 pl. עוּזֵנוּ 81:2; 3 mpl. עֻזָּמוֹ 89:18 etc.;—strength, might (usually poet., 44 times ψψ): material and physical, מִגְדַּל־עֹז Ju 9:51, and fig. (of י׳), ψ 61:4 Pr 18:10, מַחֲסִי־עֹז ψ 71:7, cf. עִיר עָז־לָנוּ Is 26:1, קִרְיַת־עֹז Pr 18:19 (sim.), 10:15 = 18:11 (fig.); עֹז טִבְטֶחָ֑ה Pr 21:22 (of a city, read poss. מעוז Toy); צוּר עֻזִּי ψ 62:8 (fig.); מְרוֹם עֻזָּהּ Je 51:53 (of Bab.), מַצְּבוֹת עֻזֵּךְ Ez 26:11 (of Tyre), cf. ψ 30:8 (reading לְהַרְרֵי־עֹז 𝔗 Hup Che Kau We; but no obj. for vb.; Gie in Bae proposes הָעֳמַדְתִּי for הֶעֱמַדְתָּה); רְקִיעַ עֻזּוֹ ψ 150:1 his mighty firmament (De Hup Kau al.; Ki AV RV Che the f. of his power, where his power dwells); בְּכָל־עֹז with all one’s might 2 S 6:14 = 1 Ch 13:8, + 2 Ch 30:21 (where so read, for MT כְּלֵי עֹז, Kau Kit cf. GFM Bu; of crocodile Jb 41:14; קוֹל עֹז ψ 68:34 a mighty voice; זְרוֹעַ עֻזּוֹ = his mighty arm (fig. of י׳) Is 62:8, cf. 51:9 ψ 89:11 Jb 26:2, גֶּשֶׁם מִטְרוֹת עֻזּוֹ Jb 37:6; מַטֵּה־עֹז, fig. of political power Je 48:17 (‖ מַקֵּל תִּפְאָרָה), Ez 19:11, 12, 14 (vine-branches, in fig.), ψ 110:2. personal, social, and political: Am 3:11, גְּאוֹן ע׳ Lv 26:19 (H), Ez 24:21; 30:6, 18; 33:28. bestowed by י׳, 1 S 2:10 (song), ψ 29:11; 68:36; 86:16; 138:3, עוֹז־לוֹ רָ֑ךְ 84:6; 138:3; so (implic.) of Zion Is 52:1. of י׳ as strength of his servants Mi 5:3; Is 49:5 ψ 81:2; 84:6, also Ex 15:2 (‖ יְשׁוּעָה) = Is 12:2 = ψ 118:14, cf. Is 45:24, עֹז יְשׁוּעָתִי ψ 140:8, תִּפְאֶרֶת עֻזָּמוֹ 89:18; sometimes as their stronghold, for defence Je 16:19 (+ מָעֻזִּי), ψ 28:7 (+ מָגִנִּי), v 8 (‖ מָעוֹז). 46:2 (+ מַחֲסֶה), 59:10 (reading עֻזִּי, v. Comm. and Ginsbmargin; ‖ מִשְׂגַּבִּי), v 18 (‖ id.), cf. Pr 14:26 (‖ מַחְסֶה); v. also sub 1.—Pr 24:5 v. עַז. might of י׳ (especially ψψ): as essential attribute Hb 3:4; Jb 12:16 ψ 62:12; 63:3; 68:35; 93:1; 96:6 = 1 Ch 16:27; עֹז מֶלֶךְ ψ 99:4 (title of י׳). theme of ascription in praise ψ 29:1 (c. יָהַב) = 96:7 = 1 Ch 16:28, ψ 59:17 (c. שִׁיר), 68:35 (c. נָתַן). exerted for his people and against his foes Ex 15:13 (song), ψ 21:2 (‖ יְשוּעָה), v 14; 66:3; 68:29 + v 29 (reading עוֹז for עוּזָּה, v. עזז), 74:13; 77:15; 78:26; 105:4 = 1 Ch 16:11, Ezr 8:22 (+ אַפּוֹ), cf. עֹז אַפֶּ֑ךָ ψ 90:11. manifested in connex. with the ark, אֲרוֹן עֻזֶּ֑ךָ 2 Ch 6:41 = ψ 132:8, עֹז = ark 78:61. עֹז פָּנָיו = boldness, impudence Ec 8:1, changed by wisdom. Du; > praise Vrss Bae.—מָעוֹז v. עוז.

### כּוֹנָנְתָּה
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: כּוּן  

vb. prob. be firm, only in der. conj. (NH Aramaic כּוּן, in der. conj., כִּיוֵּן arrange, direct; ܟܰܘܶܢ put right, correct; Assyrian kânu, be firm, right DlHWB 321; Arabic كَانَ (med. و) exist, occur, be; so Ethiopic ኮነ Sab. כון DHM ZMG 1875, 599, Ph. כון be (often));— Pf. (on 3 ms. v. **Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: כּוּן²

### אֱנוֹשׁ
**Lexicon**: Klein Dictionary
**Vocalized**: אֱנוֹשׁ  

(coll.) people, mankind.

**Etymology**: [Related to Aram. אֱנָשׁ, Syr. אֲנָשָׁא, Ugar. a̧nsh (= to be manly), Arab. ’ins (= mankind), ’unas, nas (= men), Akka. nishu (= people, nation), nishē (= people), tēnishēti, pl. (= human race, mankind). The above words are prob. related to אנשׁ ᴵ (= to be weak).]  

**Derivatives**: Derivatives: אנשׁ ᴵᴵ, אֱנוֹשׁוּת, אֱנוֹשִׁי. cp. אֲנָשִׁים, אֱנָשֵׁי, נָשִׁים.  

---

### אֱנוֹשׁ
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: אֱנוֹשׁ  

n.m. Jb 15:14 man, mankind, mostly poet. Is 13:12 (‖ אָדָם) 56:2 (‖ בֶּן־אדם); Je 20:10 א׳ שְׁלֹמִי man of my peace, i.e. my friend. coll. men Is 24:6; 33:8; 51:7 ψ 66:12; = men in general, ordinary men ψ 73:5 cf. חֶרֶט א׳ i.e. a common stylus Is 8:1 (v. אַמַּת אִישׁ Dt 3:11). man, mankind Dt 32:26; Jb 7:1; 14:19; 28:4, 13; 32:8; 33:26; 36:25 (‖ אָדָם & אֲנָשִׁים v 24) ψ 56:2; especially opp. God Jb 4:17 (‖ גֶּבֶר) 7:17; 9:2; 10:4, 5; 15:14; 25:4; 33:12; 2 Ch 14:10; ψ 8:5 (‖ בֶּן־אָדָם) so Is 51:12; ψ 9:20, 21, 90:3 (‖ בְּנֵי־אָדָם); 103:15; בֶּן־א׳ 144:3 (‖ אָדָם); cf. כִּי־א׳ רִמָּה וּבֶן־אָדָם תּוֹלֵעָה Jb 25:6; א׳ מִן־הָאָרֶץ ψ 10:18; כָּל־לְבַב א׳ Is 13:7 cf. ψ 104:15(×2). אֲנָשִׁים v. אִושׁ (p. 35).

### מֵאֱלֹהִים
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: אֱלֹהִים  

n.m.pl. (f. 1 K 11:33; on number of occurrences of אֵל, אֱלוֹהַּ, אֱלֹהִים cf. also Nesl.c.) pl. in number. rulers, judges, either as divine representatives at sacred places or as reflecting divine majesty and power: האלהים Ex 21:6 (Onk 𝔖, but τὸ κριτήριον τοῦ Θεοῦ 𝔊) 22:7, 8; אלהים 22:8, 27 (𝔗 Ra AE Ew RVm; but gods, 𝔊 Josephus Philo AV; God, Di RV; all Covt. code of E) cf. 1 S 2:25 v. Dr.; Ju 5:8 (Ew, but gods 𝔊; God 𝔖 BarHeb.; יהוה 𝔙 Be) ψ 82:1, 6 (De Ew Pe; but angels Bl Hup) 138:1 (𝔖 𝔗 Rab Ki De; but angels 𝔊 Calv; God, Ew; gods, Hup Pe Che). divine ones, superhuman beings including God and angels ψ 8:6 (De Che Br; but angels 𝔊 𝔖 𝔗 Ew; God, RV and most moderns) Gn 1:27 (if with Philo 𝔗 Jer De Che we interpret נעשה as God’s consultation with angels; cf. Jb 38:7). angels ψ 97:7 (𝔊 𝔖 Calv; but gods, Hup De Pe Che); cf. בני (ה)אלהים = (the) sons of God, or sons of gods = angels Jb 1:6; 2:1; 38:7 Gn 6:2, 4 (J; so 𝔊 Bks. of Enoch & Jubilees Philo Jude v 6; 2 Pet 2:4 JosAnt. i, 3, 1, most ancient fathers and modern critics; against usage are sons of princes, mighty men, Onk and Rab.; sons of God, the pious, Theod Chrys Jer Augustine Luther Calv Hengst; 𝔊L read οἱ υἱοὶ τοῦ Θεοῦ), cf. בני אלים. gods האלהים Ex 18:11; 22:19 (E) 1 S 4:3; 2 Ch 2:4 ψ 86:3; אלהי האלהים the God of gods, supreme God Dt 10:17 ψ 136:2; אלהים Ex 32:1, 23 (JE) Ju 9:13; אלהים אחרים other gods Ex 20:3; 23:13 Jos 24:2, 16 (E) Dt 31:18, 20 (JE) 5:7 + (17 times in D, not P) Ju 2:12, 17, 19; 10:13 1 S 8:8; 26:19 1 K 9:6, 9 (= 2 Ch 7:19, 22) 11:4, 10; 14:9 2 K 5:17; 17:35, 37, 38; 22:17 (= 2 Ch 34:25) 2 Ch 28:25 Je 1:16 + (18 times Je) Ho 3:1; אלהי (ה)נכר foreign gods Gn 35:2, 4 Jos 24:20, 23 (E) Dt 31:16 (JE) Ju 10:16 1 S 7:3 2 Ch 33:15 Je 5:19; א׳ נחור (v. 4 b); א׳ מצרים Ex 12:12 (P) Je 43:12, 13; א׳ האמרי Jos 24:15 (E) Ju 6:10; א׳ ארם etc. Ju 10:6; אלהים מעשה ידי Dt 4:28; א׳ הגוים gods of the nations 2 K 18:33; 19:12 Dt 29:17; 2 Ch 32:17, 19 Is 36:18; 37:12; א׳ העמים Dt 6:14; 13:8 Ju 2:12 ψ 96:5 1 Ch 5:25; 16:26; 2 Ch 32:13, 14; א׳ כסף Ex 20:23 (E); א׳ זהב Ex 20:23 (E) 32:31 (JE); א׳ מַסֵּכָה Ex 34:17 (J) Lv 19:4 (H). Pl. intensive. god or goddess, always with sf. 1 S 5:7 (Dagon), Ju 11:24 (Chemosh), 1 K 18:24 (Baal), Ju 9:27 Dn 1:2(×2); or cstr. לעשתרת א׳ צדנים לכמוש א׳ מואב to Ashtoreth goddess of the Zidonians, Chemosh god of Moab, etc. 1 K 11:33; א׳ הארץ god of the land 2 K 17:26(×2), 27, and so the Syrians suppose that Yahweh is a mountain-god and not a god of valleys 1 K 20:28. godlike one Ex 4:16 (J; Moses in relation to Aaron), Ex 7:1 (P; in relation to Pharaoh), 1 S 28:13 (the shade of Samuel), ψ 45:7 (the Messianic king, O God, 𝔊 𝔖 Jer, most scholars ancient and modern, but thy throne is God’s = God’s throne AE Ki Thes Ew Hup, cf. 1 Ch 28:5). works of God, or things specially belonging to him (vid. אֵל 5) הר אלהים ψ 68:16; Ez 28:14, 16; אש אלהים Jb 1:16; גן (ה)אלהים Ez 28:13; 31:8, 9. God (vid. 3 & 4). הָאֱלֹהִים the (true) God, י׳ הוא האלהים Yahweh is (the) God Dt 4:35, 39; 7:9 1 K 8:60; 18:39(×2) 2 Ch 33:13 הוא האלהים 1 K 18:24 Is 45:18; י׳ האלהים Jos 22:34 (P?) 1 K 18:21; 2 Ch 32:16; אתה הוא האלהים 2 S 7:28; 1 K 18:37; 2 K 19:15; 1 Ch 17:26; Is 37:16; Ne 9:7; האלהים as subj. or obj. is used in E 33 times, Chr 38 times, Ec 31 times, Jon 5 times, elsewhere Gn 5:22, 24; 6:9, 11 (sources of P) 17:18 (P) Jos 22:34 (P?) ### וַתְּחַסְּרֵהוּ
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: חָסֵר  

vb. lack, need, be lacking, decrease (v. Lag BN 143; NH חִסֵּר cause to lack or fail, diminish (act.), and deriv.; Aramaic חֲסַר, ܚܣܰܪ, want, lack, and deriv., cf. Ph. מחסר, v. מחסור infr.; Arabic حَسَرَ remove, strip off; disappear, retire (of water), fail (of sight), etc.; perhaps also Ethiopic ኀስረ: be inferior, worthless, diminished Di 590 and deriv.)— Pf. 3 ms. ח׳ 1 K 17:16; 2 ms. חָסַרְתָּ Dt 2:7; 3 pl. חָסֵ֑רוּ Ne 9:21; 1 pl. חָסַרְנוּ Je 44:18; Impf. יֶחְסַר Dt 15:8 + 2 times; יֶחְסָ֑ר Pr 31:11 Ec 9:8; 3 fs. תֶּחְסָ֑ר 1 K 17:14; Pr 13:25; 2 ms. תֶּחְסַר Dt 8:9; 1 s. אֶחְסָ֑ר ψ 23:1; pl. יַחְסְרוּ ψ 34:11; Ez 4:17; וַיַּחְסְרוּ Gn 8:3; יַחְסְרוּן Gn 18:28; Inf. cstr. חֲסַר Pr 10:21 Ew § 238 a, cf. Ges §45. 1 a; abs. חָסוֹר Gn 8:5; Pt. חָסֵר 1 K 11:22; Ec 10:3 (v. חָסֵר infr.);— lack: c. acc. Gn 18:28 (J) perchance the fifty righteous lack five, Dt 2:7 thou hast not lacked anything, 8:9; Je 44:18; 1 K 11:22; Ez 4:17 ψ 34:11; Pr 31:11, cf. also בַּחֲסַר־לֵב Pr 10:21 by lacking intelligence (sense), (Di puts here Dt 15:8 v. infr.) abs. be in want, want ψ 23:1; Pr 13:25; Ne 9:21. be lacking, מַחְסֹרוֹ אֲשֶׁר יֶח׳ לוֹ Dt 15:8 his lack (i.e. thing needed), which is lacking to him (possible also is, which he lacks for himself, so Di, v. supr.); שֶׁמֶן עַל־רֹאְשְׁךָ אַל־יֶח׳ Ec 9:8 oil on thy head let it not be lacking; v. also abs. Is 51:14; Ct 7:3, and לִבּוֹ חָסֵר Ec 10:3 his sense is lacking; of jar of oil 1 K 17:14, 16 (abs.) by meton. for the oil itself (cf. ‖ כלה be consumed, exhausted). diminish, decrease, of waters Gn 8:3 (P), cf. הָלוֹךְ וְחָסוֹר v 5 (P), waters continually diminished (v. הלך supr. p. 233b). cause to lack, c. acc. pers., Impf. 2 ms. sf. וַתְּחַסְּרֵהוּ מעט מֵאֱלהים ψ 8:6 and thou didst make him lack little of God; Pt. וּמְחַסֵּר את־נפשׁי מִטּוֹבָה Ec 4:8 for whom am I labouring and depriving myself of good things? Pf. הֶחֱסִיר Ex 16:18; Impf. יַחְסִיר Is 32:6;—cause to be lacking, fail, c. acc. rei מַשְׁקֵה צָמֵא יַח׳ Is 32:6; the drink of the thirsty he causeth to fail (‖ לְהָרִיק נפשׁ רָעֵב); abs. Ex 16:18 (P), he that gathered little caused no lack.

### וַתְּחַסְּרֵהוּ
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: חָסֵר²  

adj. needy, lacking, in want of—ח׳ abs. Ec 6:2; cstr. חֲסַר 1 S 21:16 + 13 times;—needy, in want of ח׳ משׁגעים אני 1 S 21:16 am I in want of madmen? 2 S 3:29 in need of bread, so Pr 12:9; usually חֲסַר־לֵב lacking understanding, sense Pr 6:32; 7:7; 9:4, 16; 10:13; 11:12; 12:11; 15:21; אָדָם ח׳־לֵב Pr 17:18; 24:30; נָּגִיד ח׳ תְּבוּנוֹת Pr 28:16; אֵינֶנּוּ חָסֵר לְנַפְשׁוֹ מִכֹּל וג׳ Ec 6:2 neither is he lacking for his soul in aught of (מן part.) all that he desireth.

### תְּעַטְּרֵהוּ
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: עָטַר  

vb. surround (NH id., and deriv.; Ph. עטרת wreath, עטר Pi. denom. crown Lzb339; Aramaic עֲטַר; Assyrian eṭéru, spare, rescue);— Impf. 2 ms. sf. כַּצִּנָּה רָצוֹן תַּעְטְרֶנּוּ ψ 5:13 as with a shield, with favour wilt thou surround him; Pt. pl. עֹטְרִים אֶל־דָּוִד 1 S 23:26 Saul and his men were surrounding (closing in) upon David.

### תְּעַטְּרֵהוּ
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: עָטַר²  

vb.denom. crown;— Pf. 3 fs. שֶׁעִטְּרָה לוֹ Ct 3:11 with which his mother crowned him (v. i. עֲטָרָה 1); elsewhere fig.: 2 ms. עִטַּרְתָּ שְׁנַת טוֹבָתֶ֑ךָ ψ 65:12 thou (י׳) hast crowned the year of thy goodness; Impf. 2 ms. sf. וְכָבוֹד וְהָדָר תְּעַטְּרֵהוּ 8:6; Pt. sf. הַמְעַטְּרֵכִי חֶסֶד וְרַחֲמִים 103:4 (of י׳). Pt. f. צֹר הַמַּעֲטִירָה Is 23:8 usually Tyre the crown-bestower (Ges Ew De CheComm. Di Du GuKau Skinner), but dub.; the crown-wearer Hi Gr (after 𝔖), so Ren CheHpt. (reading הַמְּעֻטָּרָה).

### תַּמְשִׁילֵהוּ
**Lexicon**: BDB Dictionary
**Vocalized**: מָשַׁל  

vb. represent, be like (Arabic مَثَلَ stand erect (cf. FlDe Pr 1:1), ii. effigiavit, representavit (rem alicui), v. imitate, use a verse as a proverb; مَثَلٌ description by way of comparison; Assyrian mašâlu, DlHWB 431 f.; Ethiopic መሰለ become like; Aramaic מְתַל be like, ܡܬܰܠ compare). Pf. 3 ms. נִמְשַׁל ψ 49:13, 21; 2 ms. נִמְשָׁ֑לְתָּ Is 14:10; 1 s. נִמְשַׁלְתִּי ψ 28:1; 143:7:—be like, similar, c. אֶל Is 14:10; עִם ψ 28:1 = 143:7; כְּ ψ 49:13, 21. ### תַּמְשִׁילֵהוּ

Pt. הֲלֹא מְמַשֵּׁל מְשַׁלִים הוּא Ez 20:49 is he not a maker of parables?
